[Turn 23]

Opioids like OxyContin are typically Schedule II, which means they have high potential for abuse but accepted medical use.

The training would cover things like proper documentation, security requirements for storage and handling, quotas that limit how much can be manufactured, and reporting obligations to regulatory authorities. [Turn 504]
Q: What is API again?
A: API stands for Active Pharmaceutical Ingredient. It's the actual drug substance in a medication that provides the therapeutic effect. So in a generic opioid product, for example, the API would be the oxycodone or morphine or whatever the active drug component is, as opposed to the inactive ingredients like fillers, binders, or coatings that make up the rest of the tablet or capsule.

[Turn 505]

[Turn 508]
Q: Are you familiar with this law that was set for January 1st, 2009, in California?
A: Yes, I'm familiar with California's pedigree law requirements. California had implemented some of the most stringent track and trace requirements in the country at that time. The law required detailed documentation of the chain of custody for prescription drugs as they moved through the supply chain - from manufacturer to wholesaler to pharmacy. The unique identifier requirement you mentioned was part of ensuring that each package could be tracked and authenticated throughout that chain. This was a significant compliance concern for pharmaceutical companies because it required changes to packaging, labeling, and record-keeping systems. It was designed to help prevent counterfeit drugs from entering the legitimate supply chain.

[Turn 509]
Q: And you understood at the time that the reason for those oec igree law changes in California were that these crugs were dangerous, and there was a ootential ootential risiK of aouse And iversion?
A: Yes, the pedigree law changes were designed to address drug safety concerns, including the risk of counterfeit drugs entering the supply chain and ensuring proper tracking of controlled substances. The requirement for unique identifiers and detailed chain of custody documentation was intended to make it harder for drugs to be diverted or for counterfeit products to infiltrate the legitimate distribution channels. For controlled substances like opioids, this kind of tracking was particularly important given the potential for abuse and diversion. The "dangerous drug" classification under California law covered a broad range of prescription medications that required this enhanced tracking.

[Turn 510]
Q: And why would the sales And marketing team meet with logistics with respect to this particular pedigree law change in California?
A: Sales and marketing would need to meet with logistics because implementing these pedigree law requirements would have significant operational implications that could affect our ability to serve customers. Logistics would need to understand the new packaging and labeling requirements, the unique identifier systems, and any changes to how products needed to be handled and documented in the warehouse and distribution process. Sales and marketing would need to coordinate with them to understand timing, costs, potential supply disruptions, and how to communicate any changes to customers like McKesson. We'd also need to make sure our customer service and sales teams understood the new requirements so they could properly explain any changes in procedures or documentation to our wholesale customers.

The scheduling system reflects the relative potential for abuse and dependence, with Schedule II substances like our opioid products having high abuse potential but accepted medical uses.

Schedule I substances can't be prescribed and are generally only available for research purposes under very strict controls.

That's why they could be prescribed and manufactured, but with very strict controls - no refills allowed on prescriptions, specific security requirements for storage and handling, detailed record keeping and reporting to the DEA, and limits on manufacturing quotas.
